() , ?

asked the Minister of Local Government and Provincial Councils: (a) Will he inform this House, (i) of the number of roads that belong to the Nagoda Pradeshiya Sabha in Galle; (ii) the name of the each of the aforesaid roads and the length of each of them in kilometers; (iii) separately the number of kilometers on the above mentioned roads which had been tarred or laid with concrete when the relevant Pradeshiya Sabha was elected in the year 2006; (iv) separately, the number of kilometers on roads that have been tarred or laid with concrete after the relevant Pradeshiya Sabha came into power; (v) of the fund out of which each road was constructed; (vi) out of the aforesaid roads, the number of roads that are being renovated at present or will be left for construction by the end of the year 2008 as scheduled; and (vii) whether steps will be taken to construct all remaining roads by laying tar or concrete by the end of the tenure of the present Pradeshiya Sabha? (b) If not, why?

Mohamed Mussammil) . . Seconded. , . . Question put, and agreed to. () . , (.) . Whereupon THE HON. (VEN.) UDAWATTE NANDA THERO took the Chair.

( ) (The Presiding Member) .

[. . 1.02] . . . ( . . . ) (The Hon. A. M. M. Naoshaad) . . Bismillahir Rahmanir Raheem.

Venerable Presiding Member, we have before us two sets of Regulations brought under the Land Acquisition Act, No. 9 of 1950 and the Agrarian Development Act, No. 46 of 2000. As far as the Regulations under the Land Acquisition Act are concerned, I do not think that any Member in this House has any issue with the necessity for these new Regulations because it has set right an issue that has been the bone of contention of so many projects that successive governments have undertaken where

[ . . ]

2009 17 85 86

disputes have arisen with regard to the acquisition of land for public purposes. So, I believe that these new Regulations to rectify that and pay compensation at market value, taking into account the use that particular land has been put to, and also the other contents of the land, is timely. Now, I move to the next Regulation, the Regulation under the Agrarian Development Act. We know that this Government has paid a lot of attention to the promotion of agriculture under the Api Wawamu Rata Nagamu concept. We all know that when we begin to implement our objectives, what is important is that the ground work is set so that whatever new policies that are to be implemented are understood not only by the lawmakers but by those who will be applying these laws and also those who will be governed by these laws. Now, this is where we seem to have a lot of issues in this country. We know if we are to go by the Api Wawamu Rata Nagamu concept of the Mahinda Chintana, everyone, from the farmer to the lawmaker, must be aware of the issues involved in implementing this concept. For example, if we take the Agrarian Development Act, No. 46 of 2000, this speaks of the registered farmer organizations; then we have the Agrarian Development Councils which basically take the responsibility of implementation and decision making to the grass-roots level which is what is required because we have a very literate population. The farmer has so many children who are educated. Unlike in those days, the farmer is not illiterate any more. He is a literate person. Even if he cannot read and write, his children can read and write. So, when we are in that environment, empowerment has to go to the people and the Agrarian Development Act, No. 46 of 2000 did that; took the decision-making and implementation to the grass-roots level, that is the Farmers' Organization and then, the Farmers' Organization found a place in the Agrarian Development Council for that area. But, now I see an issue in this Regulation of the appointment of a Supervision Committee by the Commissioner-General under Section 25. Basically, this Regulation spells out in detail those who would form the Supervision Committee though the original Act only gave a vague description of it. Now, in the composition of this Supervision Committee, you find that the Agrarian Development Council which is the basic body that governs the agricultural activity of the area, where the Farmers' Organization is also represented, does not find a place in the Supervision Committee. So, now this is where issues will crop up because the Farmers' Organization or the Agrarian Development Council are the implementers at the grass-roots level. They are the people who carry the policy on to the land. So, when they are not represented in a succeeding regulation, when they are left out of the Supervision Committee, you are not following through. Basically, decisions being taken by the Supervision Committee or views that are expressed by the Supervision Committee will not be readily accepted by the grass-roots level organization because they are not in that body. So, you find a break. What the Act, No. 46

of 2000 started to do, the Regulations of today are breaking that chain where there is no continuity of the command process. So, I would earnestly request the Hon. Minister to ensure that under Section 25 of Regulation 5(1)(c), one of the representatives is the representative of the Agricultural Development Council. The relevant section reads, I quote:

three officers to represent such Departments, Public Corporations and Statutory bodies that deal with Agriculture related affairs and which are functioning in the relevant Agrarian Development Council area.

Then you see a continuation. There will be the Farmers' Organizations, the Agricultural Development Council and then the Supervision Committee, so that right through the farmer is aware of the basis on which decisions are made. Why I say this is that, you know by law we are trying to give or we are empowering the Commissioner-General to basically authorize the relevant crop for a relevant area. Now, in this country, we still do not have a regulated form of agriculture or we do not have a regulated form of farming. You will find that in one village you have rice as the main cultivation, then, you will have maize, you will have kurakkan, you have everything. But, what is important here is that when we try to regulate the crop to a certain area, we must ensure that everything that is required to make that venture a success should be available. First of all, the farmer should be convinced that that is the right decision. Then, the agricultural instructor should be competent. He should know the requirements for that particular crop. He should be able to help the farmer to prepare the soil for the relevant crop. Then the Agrarian Services Centre staff should ensure that there is proper coordination to enable the required inputs to reach the farmer. Now, we know that it does not happen because all these officers, the development officers who are in the Agrarian Service Centres, the agricultural instructors have become merely Government servants. In this country, the Government servant is basically very keen in attending work as late as possible and leaving work as early as possible. Now, you cannot apply this to the agricultural sector. The agricultural sector has no time frame. So first we should make sure that the officers who are picked to play these roles understand the requirements and are mentally ready to take on the responsibility. If a farmer goes to them at 5 oclock in the morning and says, Look, I have a problem in my field and I want you to see it before the sun comes up, because once the sun comes up the insect is gone. I want you to come and see it at that time. But if the officer says, Look, my work starts at 8.30 a.m., so I will come and inspect your field at 8.30 a.m." inspecting the field at 8.30 a.m. is not going to solve the problem, because the problem is not going to exist at that time. So there cannot be a limitation in the hours of work for people involved in agriculture. So, these people should be picked carefully. They should not be given these jobs because they have a qualification. A mere paper

11 - PL 003758 - (2009/03)

87 88

[ . . . ]

qualification does not bring the requirements that are needed for officers who carry out these duties. They should be available 24 hours of the day because if not, we cannot achieve the targets we are setting. I come from a purely agricultural village. My village of Sammanthurai is surrounded by over 30,000 acres of paddy and I myself am a paddy farmer. I know that if all these people who are required to partner the farmers, really play their role and take on the responsibility of assisting the farmers, we can achieve a very much higher yield than what we are getting today. I am happy that the Hon. Minister has stepped in at this time. So, what I was saying, Hon. Minister, is that in agriculture you cannot have officers who are time-bound. If there is a problem at 5 o clock in the morning in the paddy field, the officer must be there at 5 oclock and not at 8 oclock, because at 8 oclock the pest has flown away with the sun coming up. These are the issues that crop up. Now, what I am basically telling is as somebody who comes from an agricultural family, though my ancestors have all been in politics, we are basically farmers. We do not directly till the soil but still watch the whole process. So, I understand that there is much more that can be contributed by the officers involved in making this a success. Now, I took the area of Sammanthurai. We have the Agrarian Services Centres. I would invite the Hon. Minister to make an unannounced visit to these Centres and see the environment in which these centres work. They have not seen a coat of paint for years. Officers are not present when the people need them and nobody is responsible for anything. For example, take the issue of fertilizer. The fertilizer subsidy, in my view - correct me if I am wrong - should be totally managed by the Agrarian Services Centre and the officer concerned. But, in reality who is managing it? The Vel Vidane. The responsibility of managing it, from the farmer organization is passed on to that man who sometimes, is illiterate. I took up this problem at the DCC meeting in Ampara. Two seasons ago the farmers had paid for fertilizer. But, they have not received their fertilizer. They are holding on to their receipts. They are still not able to get the fertilizer because somewhere down the line, somebody has taken it. But, the farmer is holding on to the receipt. So, I feel it is not fair to penalize the innocent farmer for the wrongdoings of the officers who are involved in this process and that the DO in charge of the Agrarian Services Centre should be held responsible by the Government to make sure that the farmer who is holding that receipt gets the fertilizer, even if it is two seasons late. Then we will get on to the Agricultural Instructors; the AIs. I do not know when the soil was tested in my area. Sometimes, I feel that we are not giving the right proportion of fertilizer with regard to the soil in that area. Today with

( ) (The Presiding Member)

Hon. Member, your time is over. . . . ( . . . ) (The Hon. A. M. M. Naoshaad)

I think I will take some time from the other speakers. Today, you know that harvesting is mechanized and therefore, straw that was earlier available in one place - all of the harvesters do not have the bale attachment which can actually bale the straw into bundles - is basically strewn around the ground as the machine goes. Now, some of them burn it and some of them leave it to rot in the field. We know when that remains and if it can be properly utilized, the composition of the fertilizer mixture that we are giving can be changed if the soil is tested at random. The AI would turn around and say, Okay, if you want, you have it tested". Fine. Yes, if I am a farmer and I want to improve my crop, I need to test it. But remember, we are giving a subsidy. So, when a subsidy is given, we must make sure that what is required is given and nothing more, because anything free in this country is welcome with both hands. If the agricultural instructors and the Agrarian Service Centres involve the farmer organizations and do this random soil testing in the areas which are under their authority, I am sure we can change the composition of the fertilizer and even the quantum of fertilizer. I welcome this move to buy back paddy from those who are enjoying the subsidy. I am of the view that the total subsidized amount must be paid back by the farmer at the guaranteed price because the guaranteed price is a fair price. But, what you must understand is that in the implementation of this, there is a huge mess up. I am aware that a circular has gone out stating that if you are to get fertilizer for the next season, the Yala season, you should have given at 200 kilos an acre for the season that just passed. Unfortunately, this instruction has not gone down to the people in the manner in which it should have gone. Take for example, Ampara. In Ampara, I think the purchase of paddy did not commence till about the end of February. The GA Ampara together with the ACAS and the Regional Manager of the Paddy Marketing Board - it became a political show, I was not invited - have gone and opened a paddy store and said, Now we are buying paddy. You have to give paddy at 200 kilos an acre. They have given 4,000 bags and said, This must be bought. Fifty per cent of the harvesting was completed and this time we had so much of demand for paddy and the lorries especially from your area, Polonnaruwa, were flooding the paddy fields. From the harvesting machines it went into the lorries. Since the farmer had no advanced knowledge of what was coming and he sold his paddy. Fine! But, what became more confusing is that when this

2009 17 89 90

process started - in my understanding, today the farmer organization has to be the nucleus of this whole operation - paddy was being handed over by the farmers. In fact, about 1,200 of those bags, about 2.5 million worth of paddy, was given. Suddenly one farmer realized that in the purchasing process, officials of the Paddy Marketing Board were naming a private individual as the agent for the purchase that was taking place. So they protested and said, No way! This has to be the farmer organization and not the private individual. From that day purchasing stopped, the Paddy Marketing Board was closed up and I was told that they had got involved in election duty. They did not come back and the whole thing flopped. Farmers, even at short notice having understood that they had a duty to give back a buffer stock to the Government so that the Government can have a buffer stock and maintain the prices, though it was not officially announced in that ceremony, started doing it. But, because of the corruption in the process the whole thing stopped. Now, there is confusion. I took this matter up at the DCC. I asked the GA, How do you go about this? Farmers started giving their paddy. Then the whole process stopped. Now, there is no paddy to give and how is it going to be implemented? He made me understand saying, No, that was only a trial attempt. We did it for this season. But, this will apply from the next season onwards. I am fully supportive of that. I feel that, as I said earlier, the subsidized value should be bought back because the guaranteed price is a fair price. Rupees 1,850 that the farmer gets for a 60 kilogram bag is a fair price. It is a stabilising price. But, the commitment to this whole process must flow. There should be a stronger bond between the DOs, the AIs, the Farmers' Organizations, the Agricultural Development Committees and the whole set up. They must understand that their duty is not to just give instructions but to ensure that those instructions are followed through.

I am telling you, this fertilizer issue is a big racket. It is a big racket and must be nipped in the bud because it is not the innocent farmer who is benefiting. It is the corrupt officials down the line. They will manipulate records and they will manipulate everything. It is done from top to bottom and it is not at the distribution level. I am making a responsible statement. It involves everybody and that is why this cover-up is done so well. You can appoint audit teams, but they will come back with no findings because all of them are involved in it. What is happening? They are bleeding the Government resources. The farmer is prepared to co-operate. The farmer appreciates this and he is prepared to give back paddy but he feels that it is unfair to get him to do this when others are playing out.

So, I have given the responsibility to the ACAS Ampara to ensure that every farmer who holds the receipt and has not received fertilizer must be given that fertilizer. They cannot just keep pushing farmers away and saying, No, no, we have issued the fertilizer. If you have issued the fertilizer it has to go to the farmer. You

cannot say, We have given it to the Farmers Organization or you cannot say that you have given it to the Vel Vidanes. No way! So, I humbly request the Hon. Minister to look into this because that is what spoils the good efforts that the Government is making.

We can easily achieve self-sufficiency in rice if all these institutions work together. Since the Hon. Minister is here, I just pointed out a flaw basically in this Regulation with regard to the Supervision Committee where in the Act we talk of a Farmers Organization; we talk of an Agrarian Development Council where the Farmers Organization is represented but this Agricultural Development Council is not represented in the Supervision Committee. So, I have said, under Section 5(1) (c) of this new Regulation, to make sure that one representative from the Agricultural Development Committee gets into that so that the chain of communication is maintained; the Farmers Organization, the Agricultural Development Committee and the Supervision Committee. So they are all very clear as to what is going on because all what the farmer needs is clarity. He will do the hard work. He is not expecting the officers to come and work with him in the field, but to tell him exactly what he needs to do and at what time. Lethargy has set into these two departments. If you send out handpicked officers of yours to go unannounced to these offices, you will see that the farmers are at their mercy. They are not at the mercy of the farmers, which should be. If the Government wants to succeed in the Api Wawamu - Rata Nagamu programme, every officer should be held responsible for the achievement of their district. Targets should be set for them and said, Look, if you cannot achieve this you have to explain because everything is being given. I know a lot of equipment was given but we do not know as to where it has gone; whether it has been allocated properly or whether political favours have been done. You cannot develop agriculture politically. No way! You cannot do that. You cannot play politics with agriculture because you cannot have a blue crop and a green crop. Hon. Minister, since you come from a rice-growing area, you have the experience. You know that. We can make it happen. But, the commitment has to come right down the line and ensure that the soil testing, the identification of crops, the selection of seed materials and the timing are done. Water is a major issue and people do not understand that for a paddy crop they do not need six inches of water. You do not need to flood your troughs till it overflows. No, all you need is two inches of water, the maximum. But, because that is not regulated and the officials are not deeply involved, there is so much of wastage and today you cannot waste like that.

Now, in Digamadulla, we cannot cultivate around 9,000 acres. Why? - Because we do not have enough water. But, I know that if the 300,000 -acre feet of water that is there is properly controlled, we can cultivate every inch. Now, this should not be left to the farmer and said,

91 92

[ . . . ]

Look, no water, you cannot cultivate. The officers must work, the Irrigation Department of Sri Lanka, the Department of Agriculture, the Agrarian Services Centres, they must all work together to see how we can cultivate all the lands with the water we have without saying we do not have enough water to cultivate all these lands because our objective is self-sufficiency. If we do not have the water what do you do? You have to manage with the water you have. But, they find the easy way out and say, 30,000 . Finish, their problem is solved. They are not concerned as to how this farmer is going to survive without cultivating a season. I am asking for more involvement of the officers, clarity in instructions, so that people understand exactly what they want and I make special mention to the Hon. Minister about this fertilizer issue. Please ensure that the farmers are not harassed for the coming season because it is the fault of the officers that there was no clear instruction given. Even where farmers of Samanthurai volunteered and gave almost Rs. 2.5 million worth of paddy to the stores, the racketeering that got involved with the agency stopped the whole process and nullified the effort that was taken.

So I commend the Hon. Minister for bringing these new Regulations and I have pointed out the flaws that may be and request you to look into these and assure you that we, as Representatives of the people, will support any meaningful step that is taken to increase production in this country.

Thank you.

[. . 1.33]

WRITTEN ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

() , ? asked the Minister of Local Government and Provincial Councils, : (a) Will he inform this House (i) of the number of roads that belong to the Galle Urban Council; (ii) the name of the each of the aforesaid roads and the length of each of them in kilometers;

(iii) separately the number of kilometers on the above mentioned roads which had been tarred or laid with concrete when the relevant Urban Council was elected in the year 2006; (iv) separately, the number of kilometers on roads that have been tarred or laid with concrete after the relevant Urban Council came into power; (v) of the fund out of which each road was con-structed; (vi) out of the aforesaid roads, the number of roads that are being renovated at present or will be left for construction by the end of the year 2008 as scheduled; and (vii) whether steps will be taken to construct all remaining roads by laying tar or concrete by the end of the tenure of the present Urban Council? (b) If not, why?

Subscriptions : The annual subscription for Official Report of Hansard is Rs. 2,178. A single copy of Hansard is available for Rs. 18.15. (Postage Rs. 2.50) Copies can be obtained by remitting in advance an annual subscription fee to the SUPERINTENDENT, GOVERNMENT PUBLICATIONS BUREAU, No. 132, Maya Avenue, Kirulapone, Colombo 5. The fee should reach him on or before November 30 each year . Late applications for subscriptions will not be accepted.

.
